<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/250px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/375px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg/500px-Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jiangyin_wenmiao_dachengdian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">Temple of Confucius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jiangyin" title="Jiangyin">Jiangyin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wuxi" title="Wuxi">Wuxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>. This is a <i>wÃ©nmiÃ o</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æåº</span></span>), that is to say a temple where <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> is worshipped as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_gods_and_immortals" title="Chinese gods and immortals"><i>WÃ©ndÃ¬</i>, "God of Culture"</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æå¸</span></span>).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3610" data-file-width="5415" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg/250px-Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg/375px-Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg/500px-Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Datong_Wenmiao_2013.08.29_11-33-25.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gates of the <i>wÃ©nmiÃ o</i> of <a href="/wiki/Datong" title="Datong">Datong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Confucianism</b>, also known as <b>Ruism</b> or <b>Ru classicism</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a system of thought and behavior originating in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>. Variously described as tradition, a philosophy, a <a href="/wiki/Religious_Confucianism" title="Religious Confucianism">religion</a>, a humanistic or rationalistic religion, a way of governing, or simply a way of life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Confucianism developed from what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a> from the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (551â479 BCE).
</p><p>Confucius considered himself a transmitter of cultural values inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia</a> (c. 2070â1600 BCE), <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> (c. 1600â1046 BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou dynasties</a> (c.â1046â771 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFung2008163_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFung2008163-3">[3]</a></sup> Confucianism was suppressed during the <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalist</a> and autocratic <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221â206 BCE), but survived. During the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (206 BCEâ220 CE), Confucian approaches edged out the "proto-Taoist" <a href="/wiki/Huang%E2%80%93Lao" title="HuangâLao">HuangâLao</a> as the official ideology, while the emperors mixed both with the realist techniques of Legalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lin_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lin-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Confucian revival began during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618â907 CE). In the late Tang, Confucianism developed in response to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and was reformulated as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>. This reinvigorated form was adopted as the basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_exams" title="Imperial exams">imperial exams</a> and the core philosophy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-officials" title="Scholar-officials">scholar official class</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960â1297). The abolition of the examination system in 1905 marked the end of official Confucianism. The intellectuals of the <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a> of the early twentieth century blamed Confucianism for China's weaknesses. They searched for new doctrines to replace Confucian teachings; some of these new ideologies include the "<a href="/wiki/Three_Principles_of_the_People" title="Three Principles of the People">Three Principles of the People</a>" with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912â1949)">Republic of China</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>. In the late twentieth century, the Confucian <a href="/wiki/Work_ethic" title="Work ethic">work ethic</a> has been credited with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_East_Asia" title="Economy of East Asia">East Asian economy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lin_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lin-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>With particular emphasis on the importance of the family and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_harmony" title="Social harmony">social harmony</a>, rather than on an otherworldly source of spiritual values,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFingarette19721â2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFingarette19721â2-5">[5]</a></sup> the core of Confucianism is <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juergensmeyer_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juergensmeyer-6">[6]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Fingarette" title="Herbert Fingarette">Herbert Fingarette</a>'s conceptualisation of Confucianism as a philosophical system which regards "the <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secular</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFingarette1972_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFingarette1972-7">[7]</a></sup> Confucianism transcends the dichotomy between religion and humanism, considering the ordinary activities of human lifeâand especially human relationshipsâas a manifestation of the sacred,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler201412_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler201412-8">[8]</a></sup> because they are the expression of humanity's moral nature (<i>xÃ¬ng</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ§</span></span>), which has a transcendent anchorage in <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Heaven</a> (<i>TiÄn</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201034â36_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201034â36-9">[9]</a></sup> While <i>TiÄn</i> has some characteristics that overlap the category of godhead, it is primarily an <i>impersonal</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_(philosophy)" title="Absolute (philosophy)">absolute principle</a>, like the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">DÃ o</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é</span></span>) or the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i>. Confucianism focuses on the practical order that is given by a this-worldly awareness of the <i>TiÄn</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Confucian liturgy (called <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> <i>rÃº</i>, or sometimes <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">æ­£ç»</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">æ­£çµ±</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhÃ¨ngtÇng</span></i>, meaning '<a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">orthopraxy</a>') led by Confucian priests or "sages of rites" (<span lang="zh-Hans">ç¤¼ç</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¦®ç</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">lÇshÄng</span></i>) to worship the gods in public and ancestral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_temple" title="Chinese temple">Chinese temples</a> is preferred on certain occasions, by Confucian religious groups and for civil religious rites, over Taoist or popular ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The worldly concern of Confucianism rests upon the belief that human beings are fundamentally good, and teachable, improvable, and perfectible through personal and communal endeavor, especially <a href="/wiki/Self-cultivation" title="Self-cultivation">self-cultivation</a> and self-creation. Confucian thought focuses on the cultivation of virtue in a morally organised world. Some of the basic Confucian ethical concepts and practices include <i>rÃ©n</i>, <i>yÃ¬</i>, and <i>lÇ</i>, and <i>zhÃ¬</i>. <i>RÃ©n</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä»</span></span>, 'benevolence' or 'humaneness') is the essence of the human being which manifests as compassion. It is the virtue-form of Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010102_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010102-12">[12]</a></sup> <i>YÃ¬</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ä¹</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¾©</span>) is the upholding of righteousness and the moral disposition to do good. <i>LÇ</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ç¤¼</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¦®</span>) is a system of ritual norms and propriety that determines how a person should properly act in everyday life in harmony with the law of Heaven. <i>ZhÃ¬</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æº</span></span>) is the ability to see what is right and fair, or the converse, in the behaviors exhibited by others. Confucianism holds one in contempt, either passively or actively, for failure to uphold the cardinal moral values of <i>rÃ©n</i> and <i>yÃ¬</i>.
</p><p>Traditionally, cultures and countries in the <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian cultural sphere</a> are strongly influenced by Confucianism, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Taiwan" title="Culture of Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Korea" title="Culture of Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Vietnam" title="Culture of Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, as well as various territories settled predominantly by <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese people</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Singapore" title="Culture of Singapore">Singapore</a>. Today, it has been credited for shaping <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> societies and <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese communities</a>, and to some extent, other parts of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In the last decades there have been talks of a "Confucian Revival" in the academic and the scholarly community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and there has been a grassroots proliferation of various types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_churches" title="Confucian churches">Confucian churches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015''passim''_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015''passim''-17">[17]</a></sup> In late 2015, many Confucian personalities formally established a national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongshenghui" title="Kongshenghui">Confucian Church</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans">å­å£ä¼</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">å­èæ</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">KÇngshÃ¨nghuÃ¬</span></i>) in China to unify the many Confucian congregations and civil society organisations.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:208px;max-width:208px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ACC-L30678.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="106" data-file-width="106" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ACC-L30678.svg/100px-ACC-L30678.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ACC-L30678.svg/150px-ACC-L30678.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/ACC-L30678.svg/200px-ACC-L30678.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Large_seal_script" title="Large seal script">Large seal</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ACC-s05667.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="153" data-file-width="153" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ACC-s05667.svg/100px-ACC-s05667.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ACC-s05667.svg/150px-ACC-s05667.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/ACC-s05667.svg/200px-ACC-s05667.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Small_seal_script" title="Small seal script">Small seal</a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Older versions of the grapheme <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> <i>rÃº</i>, meaning "scholar", "refined one", "Confucian". It is composed of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">äºº</span></span> <i>rÃ©n</i> ("person") and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é</span></span> <i>xÅ«</i> ("to await"), itself composed of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é¨</span></span> <i>yÇ</i> ("rain", "instruction") and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">è</span></span> <i>Ã©r</i> (glossed as "sky"). According to <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xinzhong_Yao" title="Xinzhong Yao">Yao Xinzhong</a>, they were the official shaman-priests (<i><a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)">wu</a></i>) experts in rites and astronomy of the Shang, and later Zhou, dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao200019_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao200019-18">[18]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Strictly speaking, there is no term in Chinese which directly corresponds to "Confucianism". In the Chinese language, the character <i>rÃº</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> meaning "scholar" or "learned" or "refined man" is generally used both in the past and the present to refer to things related to Confucianism. The character <i>rÃº</i> in ancient China had diverse meanings. Some examples include "to tame", "to mould", "to educate", "to refine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eno_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eno-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 190â197">:â190â197â</span></sup> Several different terms, some of which with modern origin, are used in different situations to express different facets of Confucianism, including:
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%92" title="wikt:å">å</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%B6" title="wikt:å®¶">å®¶</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">RÃºjiÄ</span></i> â "<i>ru</i> school of thought";</li>
<li><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%92" title="wikt:å">å</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%95%99" title="wikt:æ">æ</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">RÃºjiÃ o</span></i> â "<i>ru</i> religion" in the sense of "<i>ru</i> doctrine";</li>
<li><span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%92" title="wikt:å">å</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%A6" title="wikt:å­¦">å­¦</a></span>; <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%84%92" title="wikt:å">å</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%B8" title="wikt:å­¸">å­¸</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">RÃºxuÃ©</span></i> â "Ruology" or "<i>ru</i> learning";</li>
<li><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%94" title="wikt:å­">å­</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%95%99" title="wikt:æ">æ</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">KÇngjiÃ o</span></i> â "Confucius's doctrine";</li>
<li><span lang="zh"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%94" title="wikt:å­">å­</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%B6" title="wikt:å®¶">å®¶</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BA%97" title="wikt:åº">åº</a></span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">KÇngjiÄdiÃ n</span></i> â "Kong family's business", a pejorative phrase used in the <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Three of them use <i>rÃº</i>. These names do not use the name "Confucius" at all, but instead focus on the ideal of the Confucian man. The use of the term "Confucianism" has been avoided by some modern scholars, who favor "Ruism" and "Ruists" instead. Robert Eno argues that the term has been "burdened... with the ambiguities and irrelevant traditional associations". Ruism, as he states, is more faithful to the original Chinese name for the school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eno_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eno-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">:â7â</span></sup>
</p><p>The term "Traditionalist" has been suggested by David Schaberg to emphasize the connection to the past, its standards, and inherited forms, in which Confucius himself placed so much importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> This translation of the word <i>rÃº</i> is followed by e.g. Yuri Pines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Youguang" title="Zhou Youguang">Zhou Youguang</a>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> <i>rÃº</i> originally referred to shamanic methods of holding rites and existed before Confucius's times, but with Confucius it came to mean devotion to propagating such teachings to bring civilisation to the people. Confucianism was initiated by the disciples of Confucius, developed by <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> (c. 372â289 BCE) and inherited by later generations, undergoing constant transformations and restructuring since its establishment, but preserving the principles of humaneness and righteousness at its core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhou20121_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhou20121-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Five_Classics_.28.E4.BA.94.E7.B6.93.2C_W.C7.94j.C4.ABng.29_and_the_Confucian_vision"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_Classics_(äºç¶,_WÇjÄ«ng)_and_the_Confucian_vision">Five Classics (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">äºç¶</span></span>, <i>WÇjÄ«ng</i>) and the Confucian vision</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confucius,_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County,_Shandong_province,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg/220px-Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg/330px-Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg/440px-Confucius%2C_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County%2C_Shandong_province%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confucius,_fresco_from_a_Western_Han_tomb_of_Dongping_County,_Shandong_province,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> in a fresco from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> tomb in <a href="/wiki/Dongping_County" title="Dongping County">Dongping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a></div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, Confucius was thought to be the author or editor of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Books_and_Five_Classics" title="Four Books and Five Classics">Five Classics</a> which were the basic texts of Confucianism. The scholar <a href="/wiki/Xinzhong_Yao" title="Xinzhong Yao">Yao Xinzhong</a> allows that there are good reasons to believe that Confucian classics took shape in the hands of Confucius, but that "nothing can be taken for granted in the matter of the early versions of the classics". Professor Yao says that perhaps most scholars today hold the "pragmatic" view that Confucius and his followers, although they did not intend to create a system of classics, "contributed to their formation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYao200052â54_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYao200052â54-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The scholar <a href="/wiki/Tu_Weiming" title="Tu Weiming">Tu Weiming</a> explains these classics as embodying "five visions" which underlie the development of Confucianism:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classic_of_Change" title="Classic of Change">Classic of Change</a> or <i>Book of Changes,</i> generally held to be the earliest of the classics, shows a metaphysical vision which combines divinatory art with numerological technique and ethical insight; philosophy of change sees cosmos as interaction between the two energies yin and yang; universe always shows organismic unity and dynamism.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Classic of Poetry</a> or <i>Book of Songs</i> is the earliest anthology of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_poetry" title="Chinese poetry">Chinese poems</a> and songs. It shows the poetic vision in the belief that poetry and music convey common human feelings and mutual responsiveness.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a> or <i>Book of History</i> Compilation of speeches of major figures and records of events in ancient times embodies the political vision and addresses the kingly way in terms of the ethical foundation for humane government. The documents show the sagacity, filial piety, and work ethic of Yao, Shun, and Yu. They established a political culture which was based on responsibility and trust. Their virtue formed a covenant of social harmony which did not depend on punishment or coercion.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Rites" title="Book of Rites">Book of Rites</a> describes the social forms, administration, and ceremonial rites of the Zhou Dynasty. This social vision defined society not as an adversarial system based on contractual relations but as a community of trust based on social responsibility. The <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">four functional occupations</a> are cooperative (farmer, scholar, artisan, merchant).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals">Spring and Autumn Annals</a> chronicles the period to which it gives its name, <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> (771â476 BCE), from the perspective of Confucius's home state of <a href="/wiki/Lu_(state)" title="Lu (state)">Lu</a>. These events emphasise the significance of collective memory for communal self-identification, for reanimating the old is the best way to attain the new.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrines">Doctrines</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_and_theology">Theory and theology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg/170px-%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg/255px-%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg/340px-%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%A4%A9-oracle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracular</a> version of the grapheme for <i>TiÄn</i>, representing a man with a head informed by the north <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">celestial pole</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_and_p._3,_Vol._III,_for_the_graphic_interpretation_of_the_character_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_and_p._3,_Vol._III,_for_the_graphic_interpretation_of_the_character-25">[25]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religious_Confucianism" title="Religious Confucianism">Religious Confucianism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_theology" title="Confucian theology">Confucian theology</a></div>
<p>Confucianism revolves around the pursuit of the unity of the individual self and the God of Heaven (<i>TiÄn</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span>), or, otherwise said, around the relationship between humanity and Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler201410_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler201410-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010100_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010100-27">[27]</a></sup> The principle of Heaven (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Neo-Confucianism)" title="Li (Neo-Confucianism)">LÇ</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ç</span></span> or <i><a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">DÃ o</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é</span></span>), is the order of the creation and the source of divine authority, <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> in its structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010100_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010100-27">[27]</a></sup> Individuals may realise their humanity and become one with Heaven through the contemplation of such order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010100_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010100-27">[27]</a></sup> This transformation of the self may be extended to the family and society to create a harmonious fiduciary community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010100_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010100-27">[27]</a></sup> JoÃ«l Thoraval studied Confucianism as a diffused <a href="/wiki/Civil_religion" title="Civil religion">civil religion</a> in contemporary China, finding that it expresses itself in the widespread worship of five cosmological entities: Heaven and Earth (<i><a href="/wiki/Di_(Chinese_concept)" title="Di (Chinese concept)">Di</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å°</span></span>), the sovereign or the government (<i>jÅ«n</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span>), ancestors (<i>qÄ«n</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">è¦ª</span></span>) and masters (<i>shÄ«</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¸«</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Heaven is not some being pre-existing the temporal world. According to the scholar <a href="/wiki/Stephan_Feuchtwang" title="Stephan Feuchtwang">Stephan Feuchtwang</a>, in Chinese cosmology, which is not merely Confucian but shared by all <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese religions</a>, "the universe creates itself out of a primary chaos of material energy" (<i><a href="/wiki/Hundun" title="Hundun">hundun</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ··æ²</span></span> and <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ°£</span></span>), organising through the polarity of <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> which characterises any thing and life. Creation is therefore a continuous ordering; it is not a <i><a href="/wiki/Creatio_ex_nihilo" title="Creatio ex nihilo">creation <i>ex nihilo</i></a>.</i> "Yin and yang are the invisible and visible, the receptive and the active, the unshaped and the shaped; they characterise the yearly cycle (winter and summer), the landscape (shady and bright), the sexes (female and male), and even sociopolitical history (disorder and order). Confucianism is concerned with finding "middle ways" between yin and yang at every new configuration of the world."<i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146â150_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146â150-29">[29]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>Confucianism conciliates both the inner and outer polarities of spiritual cultivation, that is to say <a href="/wiki/Self-cultivation" title="Self-cultivation">self-cultivation</a> and world redemption, synthesised in the ideal of "sageliness within and kingliness without".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010100_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010100-27">[27]</a></sup> <i>RÃ©n</i>, translated as "humaneness" or the essence proper of a human being, is the character of compassionate mind; it is the virtue endowed by Heaven and at the same time the means by which man may achieve oneness with Heaven comprehending his own origin in Heaven and therefore divine essence. In the <i>DÃ tÃ³ng shÅ«</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">å¤§åä¹¦</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">å¤§åæ¸</span>) it is defined as "to form one body with all things" and "when the self and others are not separated ... compassion is aroused".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010102_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010102-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ti.C4.81n_and_the_gods"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="TiÄn_and_the_gods"><i>TiÄn</i> and the gods</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Precessional_north_pole_(B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81)_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris,_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="522" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg/220px-Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg/330px-Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg/440px-Precessional_north_pole_%28B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81%29_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris%2C_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Precessional_north_pole_(B%C4%9Bij%C3%AD_%E5%8C%97%E6%9E%81)_in_%CE%B1_Ursae_Minoris,_drawing_a_w%C3%A0n_%E5%8D%8D_in_the_four_phases_of_time.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Like other symbols such as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauwastika" title="Sauwastika">sauwastika</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009256,_Vol._III_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009256,_Vol._III-30">[30]</a></sup> <i>wÃ n</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> ("all things") in Chinese, the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> ð­ <i><a href="/wiki/Dingir" title="Dingir">Dingir</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Anu" title="Anu">An</a></i> ("Heaven"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and also the Chinese <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å·«</span></span> <i><a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)">wÅ«</a></i> ("shaman"; in <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a> script represented by the <a href="/wiki/Cross_potent" title="Cross potent">cross potent</a> â©),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009257,_Vol._I_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009257,_Vol._I-32">[32]</a></sup> <i>TiÄn</i> refers to the northern <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">celestial pole</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">åæ¥µ</span></span> <i>BÄijÃ­</i>), the pivot and the vault of the sky with its spinning constellations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''-33">[33]</a></sup> Here is an approximate representation of the <i>TiÄnmÃ©n</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©é</span></span> ("Gate of Heaven")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> or <i>TiÄnshÅ«</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©æ¨</span></span> ("Pivot of Heaven")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> as the precessional north celestial pole, with <a href="/wiki/Polaris" title="Polaris">Î± Ursae Minoris</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a>, with the spinning <a href="/wiki/Big_Dipper" title="Big Dipper">Chariot</a> constellations in the four phases of time. According to Reza Assasi's theories, the <i>wan</i> may not only be centred in the current precessional pole at Î± Ursae Minoris, but also very near to the north <a href="/wiki/Orbital_pole" title="Orbital pole">ecliptic pole</a> if <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">Draco</a> (<i>TiÄnlÃ³ng</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©é¾</span></span>) is conceived as one of its two beams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[note 1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></div>
<p><i>TiÄn</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span>), a key concept in Chinese thought, refers to the God of Heaven, the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">northern culmen of the skies</a> and its spinning stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''-33">[33]</a></sup> earthly nature and its laws which come from Heaven, to "Heaven and Earth" (that is, "all things"), and to the awe-inspiring forces beyond human control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagen_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen-38">[37]</a></sup> There are such a number of uses in Chinese thought that it is not possible to give one translation into English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucius used the term in a mystical way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu2014_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu2014-40">[39]</a></sup> He wrote in the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i> (7.23) that Tian gave him life, and that Tian watched and judged (6.28; 9.12). In 9.5 Confucius says that a person may know the movements of the Tian, and this provides with the sense of having a special place in the universe. In 17.19 Confucius says that Tian spoke to him, though not in words. The scholar Ronnie Littlejohn warns that Tian was not to be interpreted as personal God comparable to that of the Abrahamic faiths, in the sense of an otherworldly or transcendent creator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035â36_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035â36-41">[40]</a></sup> Rather it is similar to what <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoists" title="Taoists">Taoists</a> meant by <i>Dao</i>: "the way things are" or "the regularities of the world",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagen_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen-38">[37]</a></sup> which Stephan Feuchtwang equates with the ancient Greek concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Physis" title="Physis">physis</a></i>, "nature" as the generation and regenerations of things and of the moral order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup> Tian may also be compared to the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedic</a> traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler201410_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler201410-26">[26]</a></sup> The scholar Promise Hsu, in the wake of Robert B. Louden, explained 17:19 ("What does Tian ever say? Yet there are four seasons going round and there are the hundred things coming into being. What does Tian say?") as implying that even though Tian is not a "speaking person", it constantly "does" through the rhythms of nature, and communicates "how human beings ought to live and act", at least to those who have learnt to carefully listen to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu2014_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu2014-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Duanmu_Ci" title="Duanmu Ci">Zigong</a>, a disciple of Confucius, said that Tian had set the master on the path to become a wise man (9.6). In 7.23 Confucius says that he has no doubt left that the Tian gave him life, and from it he had developed right virtue (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¾·</span></span> <i>dÃ©</i>). In 8.19 he says that the lives of the sages are interwoven with Tian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201035-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding personal <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">gods</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">shÃ©n</a></i>, energies who emanate from and reproduce the Tian) enliving nature, in the <i>Analects</i> Confucius says that it is appropriate (<span lang="zh-Hans">ä¹</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¾©</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">yÃ¬</span></i>) for people to worship (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ¬</span></span> <i>jÃ¬ng</i>) them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036-43">[42]</a></sup> though through proper <a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">rites</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ç¤¼</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¦®</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">lÇ</span></i>), implying respect of positions and discretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036-43">[42]</a></sup> Confucius himself was a ritual and <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrificial</a> master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201037_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201037-44">[43]</a></sup> Answering to a disciple who asked whether it is better to sacrifice to the god of the stove or to the god of the family (a popular saying), in 3.13 Confucius says that in order to appropriately pray gods one should first know and respect Heaven. In 3.12 he explains that religious rituals produce meaningful experiences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036â37_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201036â37-45">[44]</a></sup> and one has to offer sacrifices in person, acting in presence, otherwise "it is the same as not having sacrificed at all". Rites and sacrifices to the gods have an <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> importance: they generate good life, because taking part in them leads to the overcoming of the self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShenShun2007278â279_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShenShun2007278â279-46">[45]</a></sup> Analects 10.11 tells that Confucius always took a small part of his food and placed it on the sacrificial bowls as an offering to his <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201037_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201037-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other movements, such as <a href="/wiki/Mohism" title="Mohism">Mohism</a> which was later absorbed by Taoism, developed a more <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a> idea of Heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Feuchtwang explains that the difference between Confucianism and Taoism primarily lies in the fact that the former focuses on the realisation of the starry order of Heaven in human society, while the latter on the contemplation of the Dao which spontaneously arises in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_morality_and_ethics">Social morality and ethics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Three_Fundamental_Bonds_and_Five_Constant_Virtues" title="Three Fundamental Bonds and Five Constant Virtues">Three Fundamental Bonds and Five Constant Virtues</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg/220px-%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg/330px-%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg/440px-%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%BC%A0%E6%8C%A5%E5%85%AC%E5%A4%A7%E6%AE%BF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worship at the Great Temple of Lord Zhang Hui (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¼ æ¥å¬å¤§æ®¿</span></span> <i>ZhÄng HuÄ« gÅng dÃ diÃ n</i>), the cathedral <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_shrine" title="Ancestral shrine">ancestral shrine</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zhang_(surname)" title="Zhang (surname)">Zhang</a> lineage corporation, at their <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_home_(Chinese)" title="Ancestral home (Chinese)">ancestral home</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qinghe_County,_Hebei" title="Qinghe County, Hebei">Qinghe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG/220px-Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG/330px-Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG/440px-Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mazhan_Zhen_-_Houxian_Cun_-_P1220012.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancestral temple of the <a href="/wiki/Zeng" title="Zeng">Zeng</a> lineage and Houxian village cultural centre, <a href="/wiki/Cangnan_County" title="Cangnan County">Cangnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a></div></div></div>
<p>As explained by Stephan Feuchtwang, the order coming from Heaven preserves the world, and has to be followed by humanity finding a "middle way" between yin and yang forces in each new configuration of reality. Social harmony or morality is identified as patriarchy, which is expressed in the worship of ancestors and deified progenitors in the male line, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_shrines" title="Ancestral shrines">ancestral shrines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucian ethical codes are described as humanistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juergensmeyer_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Juergensmeyer-6">[6]</a></sup> They may be practiced by all the members of a society. Confucian ethics is characterised by the promotion of virtues, encompassed by the Five Constants, <i>WÇchÃ¡ng</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">äºå¸¸</span></span>) in Chinese, elaborated by Confucian scholars out of the inherited tradition during the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runes_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runes-48">[47]</a></sup> The Five Constants are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runes_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Runes-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ren_(Confucianism)" title="Ren (Confucianism)">RÃ©n</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä»</span></span>, benevolence, humaneness);</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yi_(Confucianism)" title="Yi (Confucianism)">YÃ¬</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ä¹</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¾©</span>, righteousness, justice);</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">LÇ</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ç¤¼</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¦®</span>, propriety, rites);</li>
<li><i>ZhÃ¬</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æº</span></span>, wisdom, knowledge);</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Xin_(philosophy)" title="Xin (philosophy)">XÃ¬n</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¿¡</span></span>, sincerity, faithfulness).</li></ul>
<p>These are accompanied by the classical <i>SÃ¬zÃ¬</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">åå­</span></span>), that singles out four virtues, one of which (YÃ¬) is included among the Five Constants:
</p>
<ul><li><i>ZhÅng</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¿ </span></span>, loyalty);</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xi%C3%A0o" title="XiÃ o">XiÃ o</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å­</span></span>, filial piety);</li>
<li><i>JiÃ©</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">è</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¯</span>, continence);</li>
<li><i>YÃ¬</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ä¹</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¾©</span>, righteousness).</li></ul>
<p>There are still many other elements, such as <i>chÃ©ng</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">è¯</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">èª </span>, honesty), <i>shÃ¹</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ</span></span>, kindness and forgiveness), <i>liÃ¡n</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å»</span></span>, honesty and cleanness), <i>chÇ</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">è»</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">æ¥</span>, shame, judge and sense of right and wrong), <i>yÇng</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span>, bravery), <i>wÄn</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">æ¸©</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">æº«</span>, kind and gentle), <i>liÃ¡ng</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">è¯</span></span>, good, kindhearted), <i>gÅng</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ­</span></span>, respectful, reverent), <i>jiÇn</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ä¿­</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">å</span>, frugal), <i>rÃ ng</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">è®©</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">è®</span>, modestly, self-effacing).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Humaneness">Humaneness</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ren_(Confucianism)" title="Ren (Confucianism)">Ren (Confucianism)</a></div>
<p><i>RÃ©n</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">ä»</span>) is the Confucian virtue denoting the good feeling a virtuous human experiences when being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altruistic" title="Altruistic">altruistic</a>. It is exemplified by a normal adult's protective feelings for children. It is considered the essence of the human being, endowed by Heaven, and at the same time the means by which man may act according to the principle of Heaven (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©ç</span></span>, <i>TiÄn lÇ</i>) and become one with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETay2010102_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETay2010102-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yan_Hui_(disciple_of_Confucius)" title="Yan Hui (disciple of Confucius)">YÃ¡n HuÃ­</a>, Confucius's most outstanding student, once asked his master to describe the rules of <i>rÃ©n</i> and Confucius replied, "one should see nothing improper, hear nothing improper, say nothing improper, do nothing improper."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Confucius also defined <i>rÃ©n</i> in the following way: "wishing to be established himself, seeks also to establish others; wishing to be enlarged himself, he seeks also to enlarge others."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another meaning of <i>rÃ©n</i> is "not to do to others as you would not wish done to yourself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Confucius also said, "<i>rÃ©n</i> is not far off; he who seeks it has already found it." <i>RÃ©n</i> is close to man and never leaves him.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rite_and_centring">Rite and centring</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2383" data-file-width="3178" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg/220px-Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg/330px-Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg/440px-Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guan_County_Temple_of_Confucius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of Confucius in <a href="/wiki/Dujiangyan_City" title="Dujiangyan City">Dujiangyan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg/220px-Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg/330px-Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg/440px-Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Korean_Confucianism-Chugyedaeje-02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Korean_Confucianism" title="Korean Confucianism">Korean Confucian</a> rite in <a href="/wiki/Jeju_Province" title="Jeju Province">Jeju</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">Li (Confucianism)</a></div>
<p><i>Li</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">ç¤¼</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¦®</span>) is a classical Chinese word which finds its most extensive use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> and post-Confucian <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Li_(Confucianism)" title="Li (Confucianism)">Li</a></i> is variously translated as "<a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rite</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>," "ratio" in the pure sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/%E1%B9%9Ata" title="á¹ta">á¹ta</a></i> ("right," "order") when referring to the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmic</a> law, but when referring to its realisation in the context of human social behaviour it has also been translated as "<a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">customs</a>", "measures" and "rules", among other terms. <i>Li</i> also means religious rites which establish relations between humanity and the gods.
</p><p>According to Stephan Feuchtwang, rites are conceived as "what makes the invisible visible", making possible for humans to cultivate the underlying order of nature. Correctly performed rituals move society in alignment with earthly and heavenly (astral) forces, establishing the harmony of the three realmsâHeaven, Earth and humanity. This practice is defined as "centring" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤®</span></span> <i>yÄng</i> or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¸­</span></span> <i>zhÅng</i>). Among all things of creation, humans themselves are "central" because they have the ability to cultivate and centre natural forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016150_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016150-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Li</i> embodies the entire web of interaction between humanity, human objects, and nature. Confucius includes in his discussions of <i>li</i> such diverse topics as learning, tea drinking, titles, mourning, and governance. <a href="/wiki/Xun_Kuang" title="Xun Kuang">Xunzi</a> cites "songs and laughter, weeping and lamentation... rice and millet, fish and meat... the wearing of ceremonial caps, embroidered robes, and patterned silks, or of fasting clothes and mourning clothes... spacious rooms and secluded halls, soft mats, couches and benches" as vital parts of the fabric of <i>li</i>.
</p><p>Confucius envisioned proper government being guided by the principles of <i>li</i>. Some Confucians proposed that all human beings may pursue perfection by learning and practising <i>li</i>. Overall, Confucians believe that governments should place more emphasis on <i>li</i> and rely much less on penal punishment when they govern.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Loyalty">Loyalty</span></h4>
<p>Loyalty (<span lang="zh">å¿ </span>, <i>zhÅng</i>) is particularly relevant for the social class to which most of Confucius's students belonged, because the most important way for an ambitious young scholar to become a prominent official was to enter a ruler's civil service.
</p><p>Confucius himself did not propose that "might makes right," but rather that a superior should be obeyed because of his moral rectitude. In addition, loyalty does not mean subservience to authority. This is because reciprocity is demanded from the superior as well. As Confucius stated "a prince should employ his minister according to the rules of propriety; ministers should serve their prince with faithfulness (loyalty)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> also said that "when the prince regards his ministers as his hands and feet, his ministers regard their prince as their belly and heart; when he regards them as his dogs and horses, they regard him as another man; when he regards them as the ground or as grass, they regard him as a robber and an enemy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Moreover, Mencius indicated that if the ruler is incompetent, he should be replaced. If the ruler is evil, then the people have the right to overthrow him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> A good Confucian is also expected to remonstrate with his superiors when necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> At the same time, a proper Confucian ruler should also accept his ministers' advice, as this will help him govern the realm better.
</p><p>In later ages, however, emphasis was often placed more on the obligations of the ruled to the ruler, and less on the ruler's obligations to the ruled. Like filial piety, loyalty was often subverted by the autocratic regimes in China. Nonetheless, throughout the ages, many Confucians continued to fight against unrighteous superiors and rulers. Many of these Confucians suffered and sometimes died because of their conviction and action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> During the Ming-Qing era, prominent Confucians such as <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yangming" title="Wang Yangming">Wang Yangming</a> promoted individuality and independent thinking as a counterweight to subservience to authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> The famous thinker Huang Zongxi also strongly criticised the autocratic nature of the imperial system and wanted to keep imperial power in check.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Confucians also realised that loyalty and filial piety have the potential of coming into conflict with one another. This may be true especially in times of social chaos, such as during the period of the Ming-Qing transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Filial_piety">Filial piety</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG/220px-%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG/330px-%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG/440px-%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%B0%9D%E7%B2%AA%E5%BF%83%E5%BF%A7.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fourteenth of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twenty-four_Filial_Exemplars" title="The Twenty-four Filial Exemplars">The Twenty-four Filial Exemplars</a></i></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">Filial piety</a></div>
<p>In Confucian philosophy, filial <a href="/wiki/Piety" title="Piety">piety</a> (<span lang="zh">å­</span>, <i>xiÃ o</i>) is a virtue of respect for one's parents and ancestors, and of the hierarchies within society: fatherâson, elderâjunior and maleâfemale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup> The Confucian classic <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Filial_Piety" title="Classic of Filial Piety">Xiaojing</a></i> ("Book of Piety"), thought to be written around the Qin-Han period, has historically been the authoritative source on the Confucian tenet of <i>xiÃ o</i>. The book, a conversation between Confucius and his disciple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeng_Shen" title="Zeng Shen">Zeng Shen</a>, is about how to set up a good society using the principle of <i>xiÃ o</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In more general terms, filial piety means to be good to one's parents; to take care of one's parents; to engage in good conduct not just towards parents but also outside the home so as to bring a good name to one's parents and ancestors; to perform the duties of one's job well so as to obtain the material means to support parents as well as carry out sacrifices to the ancestors; not be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebellious" title="Rebellious">rebellious</a>; show love, respect and support; the <a href="/wiki/Feminism_in_China" title="Feminism in China">wife</a> in filial piety must obey her husband absolutely and take care of the whole family wholeheartedly. display courtesy; ensure male heirs, uphold fraternity among brothers; wisely advise one's parents, including dissuading them from moral unrighteousness, for blindly following the parents' wishes is not considered to be <i>xiao</i>; display sorrow for their sickness and death; and carry out <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifices</a> after their death.
</p><p>Filial piety is considered a key virtue in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a>, and it is the main concern of a large number of stories. One of the most famous collections of such stories is "<a href="/wiki/The_Twenty-four_Filial_Exemplars" title="The Twenty-four Filial Exemplars">The Twenty-four Filial Exemplars</a>". These stories depict how children exercised their filial piety in the past. While China has always had a diversity of religious beliefs, filial piety has been common to almost all of them; historian Hugh D.R. Baker calls respect for the family the only element common to almost all Chinese believers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships">Relationships</span></h3>
<p>Social harmony results in part from every individual knowing his or her place in the natural order, and playing his or her part well. Reciprocity or responsibility (<i>renqing</i>) extends beyond filial piety and involves the entire network of social relations, even the respect for rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup> This is shown in the story where Duke Jing of Qi asks Confucius about government, by which he meant proper administration so as to bring social harmony.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh" style="font-size: 115%;">é½æ¯å¬åæ¿æ¼å­å­ãå­å­å°æ°ï¼ååï¼è£è£ï¼ç¶ç¶ï¼å­å­ã</span></span><br/>
The duke Jing, of Qi, asked Confucius about government. Confucius replied, "There is government, when the prince is prince, and the minister is minister; when the father is father, and the son is son."
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><i>Analects</i> 12.11 (Legge translation).</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Particular duties arise from one's particular situation in relation to others. The individual stands simultaneously in several different relationships with different people: as a junior in relation to parents and elders, and as a senior in relation to younger siblings, students, and others. While juniors are considered in Confucianism to owe their seniors reverence, seniors also have duties of benevolence and concern toward juniors. The same is true with the husband and wife relationship where the husband needs to show benevolence towards his wife and the wife needs to respect the husband in return. This theme of mutuality still exists in East Asian cultures even to this day.
</p><p>The Five Bonds are: ruler to ruled, father to son, husband to wife, elder brother to younger brother, friend to friend. Specific duties were prescribed to each of the participants in these sets of relationships. Such duties are also extended to the dead, where the living stand as sons to their deceased family. The only relationship where respect for elders isn't stressed was the friend to friend relationship, where mutual equal respect is emphasised instead. All these duties take the practical form of prescribed rituals, for instance wedding and death rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Junzi"><i>Junzi</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Junzi" title="Junzi">Junzi</a></div>
<p>The <i>junzi</i> (<span lang="zh">åå­</span>, <i>jÅ«nzÇ</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Prince" title="Prince">lord's son</a>") is a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophical</a> term often translated as "<a href="/wiki/Gentleman" title="Gentleman">gentleman</a>" or "superior person"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> and employed by <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> in the <i>Analects</i> to describe the ideal man.
</p><p>In Confucianism, the sage or wise is the ideal personality; however, it is very hard to become one of them. Confucius created the model of <i>junzi</i>, gentleman, which may be achieved by any individual. Later, <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> defined <i>junzi</i> as second only to the sage. There are many characteristics of the <i>junzi</i>: he may live in <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, he does more and speaks less, he is <a href="/wiki/Loyalty" title="Loyalty">loyal</a>, obedient and knowledgeable. The <i>junzi</i> disciplines himself. <i>Ren</i> is fundamental to become a <i>junzi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the potential leader of a nation, a son of the ruler is raised to have a superior ethical and moral position while gaining inner peace through his virtue. To Confucius, the <i>junzi</i> sustained the functions of government and social stratification through his ethical values. Despite its literal meaning, any righteous man willing to improve himself may become a <i>junzi</i>.
</p><p>On the contrary, the <i>xiaoren</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å°äºº</span></span>, <i>xiÄorÃ©n</i>, "small or petty person") does not grasp the value of virtues and seeks only immediate gains. The petty person is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egotistic" title="Egotistic">egotistic</a> and does not consider the consequences of his action in the overall scheme of things. Should the ruler be surrounded by <i>xiaoren</i> as opposed to <i>junzi</i>, his governance and his people will suffer due to their small-mindness. Examples of such <i>xiaoren</i> individuals may range from those who continually indulge in sensual and <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotional</a> pleasures all day to the <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politician</a> who is interested merely in <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celebrity" title="Celebrity">fame</a>; neither sincerely aims for the long-term benefit of others.
</p><p>The <i>junzi</i> enforces his rule over his subjects by acting virtuously himself. It is thought that his pure <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> would lead others to follow his example. The ultimate goal is that the government behaves much like a <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">family</a>, the <i>junzi</i> being a beacon of filial piety.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rectification_of_names">Rectification of names</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="1276" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg/220px-Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg/330px-Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg/440px-Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tablet_of_Confucius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priest paying homage to Confucius's tablet, c. 1900</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rectification_of_names" title="Rectification of names">Rectification of names</a></div>
<p>Confucius believed that social disorder often stemmed from failure to perceive, understand, and deal with <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>. Fundamentally, then, social disorder may stem from the failure to call things by their proper names, and his solution to this was <i>zhÃ¨ngmÃ­ng</i> (<span lang="zh">æ­£å</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhÃ¨ngmÃ­ng</span></i>; 'rectification of terms'). He gave an explanation of <i>zhengming</i> to one of his disciples.
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Zi-lu said, "The vassal of Wei has been waiting for you, in order with you to administer the government. What will you consider the first thing to be done?"<br/>
The Master replied, "What is necessary to rectify names."<br/>
"So! indeed!" said Zi-lu. "You are wide off the mark! Why must there be such rectification?"<br/>
The Master said, "How uncultivated you are, Yu! The superior man [Junzi] cannot care about the everything, just as he cannot go to check all himself!<br/>
Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â If names be not correct, language is not in accordance with the truth of things.<br/>
Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â If language be not in accordance with the truth of things, affairs cannot be carried on to success.<br/>
Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â When affairs cannot be carried on to success, proprieties and music do not flourish.<br/>
Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â When proprieties and music do not flourish, punishments will not be properly awarded.<br/>
Â Â Â Â Â Â Â Â When punishments are not properly awarded, the people do not know how to move hand or foot.<br/>
Therefore a superior man considers it necessary that the names he uses may be spoken appropriately, and also that what he speaks may be carried out appropriately. What the superior man requires is just that in his words there may be nothing incorrect."<br/>
(<i>Analects</i> XIII, 3, tr. Legge)
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xun_Zi" title="Xun Zi">Xun Zi</a> chapter (22) "On the Rectification of Names" claims the ancient sage-kings chose names (<span lang="zh">å</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mÃ­ng</span></i>) that directly corresponded with actualities (<span lang="zh">å¯¦</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shÃ­</span></i>), but later generations confused terminology, coined new nomenclature, and thus could no longer distinguish right from wrong. Since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmonious_society" title="Harmonious society">social harmony</a> is of utmost importance, without the proper rectification of names, society would essentially crumble and "undertakings [would] not [be] completed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3376" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg/220px-Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg/330px-Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg/440px-Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temple_rooftop_dragon_in_Taiwan_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a> is one of the oldest symbols of Chinese religious culture. It symbolises the supreme godhead, Di or Tian, at the north <a href="/wiki/Orbital_pole" title="Orbital pole">ecliptic pole</a>, around which it coils itself as the <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">homonymous constellation</a>.
