It is a symbol of the "protean" supreme power which has in itself both <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankenier201355_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankenier201355-66">[65]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1585" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/220px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/330px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png/440px-Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birth_Places_of_Chinese_Philosophers.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birthplaces of notable Chinese philosophers of the Hundred Schools of Thought in Zhou dynasty. Confucians are marked by triangles in dark red.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_religion_in_China" title="History of religion in China">History of religion in China</a></div>
<p>According to He Guanghu, Confucianism may be identified as a continuation of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang</a>-<a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a> (~1600â256 BCE) official religion, or the Chinese aboriginal religion which has lasted uninterrupted for three thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012105,_note_45_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012105,_note_45-67">[66]</a></sup> Both the dynasties worshipped the supreme godhead, called <i><a href="/wiki/Shangdi" title="Shangdi">Shangdi</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¸å¸</span></span> "Highest Deity") or simply <i>DÃ¬</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¸</span></span>) by the Shang and <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span> "Heaven") by the Zhou. Shangdi was conceived as the first ancestor of the Shang royal house,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743-68">[67]</a></sup> an alternate name for him being the "Supreme Progenitor" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¸ç²</span></span> <i>ShÃ ngjiÇ</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009227â228,_Vol._II_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009227â228,_Vol._II-69">[68]</a></sup> In Shang theology, the multiplicity of gods of nature and ancestors were viewed as parts of Di, and the four <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ¹</span></span> <i><a href="/wiki/Wufang_Shangdi" title="Wufang Shangdi">fÄng</a></i> ("directions" or "sides") and their <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é¢¨</span></span> <i>fÄng</i> ("winds") as his <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmic</a> will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009143â144,_Vol._II_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009143â144,_Vol._II-70">[69]</a></sup> With the Zhou dynasty, which overthrew the Shang, the name for the supreme godhead became <i><a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Tian</a></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span> "Heaven").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743-68">[67]</a></sup> While the Shang identified Shangdi as their ancestor-god to assert their claim to power by divine right, the Zhou transformed this claim into a legitimacy based on moral power, the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a>. In Zhou theology, Tian had no singular earthly progeny, but bestowed divine favour on virtuous rulers. Zhou kings declared that their victory over the Shang was because they were virtuous and loved their people, while the Shang were tyrants and thus were deprived of power by Tian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFung2008163_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFung2008163-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>John C. Didier and David Pankenier relate the shapes of both the ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> for Di and Tian to the patterns of stars in the northern skies, either drawn, in Didier's theory by connecting the constellations bracketing the north celestial pole as a square,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009103,_Vol._II_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009103,_Vol._II-71">[70]</a></sup> or in Pankenier's theory by connecting some of the stars which form the constellations of the Big Dipper and broader <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Ursa Major</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a> (Little Dipper).<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankenier2013138â148,_"Chapter_4:_Bringing_Heaven_Down_to_Earth"_72-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankenier2013138â148,_"Chapter_4:_Bringing_Heaven_Down_to_Earth"-72'>[71]</a></sup> Cultures in other parts of the world have also conceived these stars or constellations as symbols of the origin of things, the supreme godhead, divinity and royal power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_Vol._I_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009''passim''_Vol._I-73">[72]</a></sup> The supreme godhead was also identified with the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a>, symbol of unlimited power (<i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELibbrecht200743-68">[67]</a></sup> of the "protean" primordial power which embodies both <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> in unity, associated to the constellation <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">Draco</a> which winds around the north <a href="/wiki/Orbital_pole" title="Orbital pole">ecliptic pole</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPankenier201355_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPankenier201355-66">[65]</a></sup> and slithers between the Little and Big Dipper.
</p><p>By the 6th century BCE the power of Tian and the symbols that represented it on earth (architecture of cities, temples, altars and ritual cauldrons, and the Zhou ritual system) became "diffuse" and claimed by different potentates in the Zhou states to legitimise economic, political, and military ambitions. Divine right no longer was an exclusive privilege of the Zhou royal house, but might be bought by anyone able to afford the elaborate ceremonies and the old and new rites required to access the authority of Tian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviâxxxvii,_Vol._I_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviâxxxvii,_Vol._I-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides the waning Zhou ritual system, what may be defined as "wild" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é</span></span> <i>yÄ</i>) traditions, or traditions "outside of the official system", developed as attempts to access the will of Tian. The population had lost faith in the official tradition, which was no longer perceived as an effective way to communicate with Heaven. The traditions of the <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¹é</span></span> ("Nine Fields") and of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i> flourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviiâxxxviii,_Vol._I_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviiâxxxviii,_Vol._I-75">[74]</a></sup> Chinese thinkers, faced with this challenge to legitimacy, diverged in a "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_Schools_of_Thought" title="Hundred Schools of Thought">Hundred Schools of Thought</a>", each proposing its own theories for the reconstruction of the Zhou moral order.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (551â479 BCE) appeared in this period of political decadence and spiritual questioning. He was educated in Shang-Zhou theology, which he contributed to transmit and reformulate giving centrality to <a href="/wiki/Self-cultivation" title="Self-cultivation">self-cultivation</a> and agency of humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFung2008163_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFung2008163-3">[3]</a></sup> and the educational power of the self-established individual in assisting others to establish themselves (the principle of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æäºº</span></span> <i>Ã irÃ©n</i>, "loving others").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhou20122_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhou20122-76">[75]</a></sup> As the Zhou reign collapsed, traditional values were abandoned resulting in a period of moral decline. Confucius saw an opportunity to reinforce values of compassion and tradition into society. Disillusioned with the widespread vulgarisation of the rituals to access Tian, he began to preach an ethical interpretation of traditional Zhou religion. In his view, the power of Tian is immanent, and responds positively to the sincere heart driven by humaneness and rightness, decency and altruism. Confucius conceived these qualities as the foundation needed to restore socio-political harmony. Like many contemporaries, Confucius saw ritual practices as efficacious ways to access Tian, but he thought that the crucial knot was the state of <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> that participants enter prior to engage in the ritual acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviii,_Vol._I_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviii,_Vol._I-77">[76]</a></sup> Confucius amended and recodified the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">classical books</a> inherited from the Xia-Shang-Zhou dynasties, and composed the <i><a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_Annals" title="Spring and Autumn Annals">Spring and Autumn Annals</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhou20121_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhou20121-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosophers in the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, both "inside the square" (focused on state-endorsed ritual) and "outside the square" (non-aligned to state ritual) built upon Confucius's legacy, compiled in the <i><a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a></i>, and formulated the classical metaphysics that became the lash of Confucianism. In accordance with the Master, they identified mental tranquility as the state of Tian, or the One (ä¸ <i>YÄ«</i>), which in each individual is the Heaven-bestowed divine power to rule one's own life and the world. Going beyond the Master, they theorised the oneness of production and reabsorption into the cosmic source, and the possibility to understand and therefore reattain it through meditation. This line of thought would have influenced all Chinese individual and collective-political mystical theories and practices thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviiiâxxxix,_Vol._I_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDidier2009xxxviiiâxxxix,_Vol._I-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_and_liturgy">Organisation and liturgy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_Confucianism" title="Religious Confucianism">Religious Confucianism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Confucian_church" title="Confucian church">Confucian churches</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_lineage_associations" title="Chinese lineage associations">Lineage churches</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Confucius" title="Temple of Confucius">Temple of Confucius</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Confucian_ritual_religion" title="Confucian ritual religion">Confucian ritual religion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongshenghui" title="Kongshenghui">Kongshenghui</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg/220px-%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg/330px-%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg/440px-%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%9F%B3%E5%B7%9E%E5%B8%82%E5%AD%94%E5%BA%99_Liuzhou_Confucius_Temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Temple of the God of Culture (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æåº</span></span> <i>wÃ©nmiÃ o</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Liuzhou" title="Liuzhou">Liuzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> is worshiped as <i>WÃ©ndÃ¬</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æå¸</span></span>), "God of Culture"</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/220px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/330px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg/440px-Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai%2C_Wenzhou%2C_Zhejiang%2C_China_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_the_Filial_Blessing_in_Ouhai,_Wenzhou,_Zhejiang,_China_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple of the Filial Blessing (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å­ä½å®«</span></span> <i>XiÃ oyÃ²ugÅng</i>), an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_temple" title="Ancestral temple">ancestral temple</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lineage_church" title="Lineage church">lineage church</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Wenzhou" title="Wenzhou">Wenzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since the 2000s, there has been a growing identification of the Chinese intellectual class with Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2010_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2010-79">[78]</a></sup> In 2003, the Confucian intellectual Kang Xiaoguang published a manifesto in which he made four suggestions: Confucian education should enter official education at any level, from elementary to high school; the state should establish Confucianism as the state religion by law; Confucian religion should enter the daily life of ordinary people through standardisation and development of doctrines, rituals, organisations, churches and activity sites; the Confucian religion should be spread through non-governmental organisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2010_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2010-79">[78]</a></sup> Another modern proponent of the institutionalisation of Confucianism in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_church" title="State church">state church</a> is <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing_(Confucian)" title="Jiang Qing (Confucian)">Jiang Qing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012175_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012175-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, the Center for the Study of Confucian Religion was established,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2010_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2010-79">[78]</a></sup> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guoxue" title="Guoxue">guoxue</a></i> started to be implemented in public schools on all levels. Being well received by the population, even Confucian preachers have appeared on television since 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2010_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2010-79">[78]</a></sup> The most enthusiastic New Confucians proclaim the uniqueness and superiority of Confucian Chinese culture, and have generated some popular sentiment against Western cultural influences in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang2010_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-Yang2010-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea of a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_Church" title="Confucian Church">Confucian Church</a>" as the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> has roots in the thought of <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a>, an exponent of the early New Confucian search for a regeneration of the social relevance of Confucianism, at a time when it was de-institutionalised with the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> and the Chinese empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012174_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012174-81">[80]</a></sup> Kang modeled his ideal "Confucian Church" after European national Christian churches, as a hierarchic and centralised institution, closely bound to the state, with local church branches, devoted to the worship and the spread of the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012174_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012174-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contemporary China, the Confucian revival has developed into various interwoven directions: the proliferation of Confucian schools or academies (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuyuan" title="Shuyuan">shuyuan</a></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¹¦é¢</span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012175_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012175-80">[79]</a></sup> the resurgence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_rites" title="Confucian rites">Confucian rites</a> (<i>chuÃ¡ntÇng lÇyÃ­</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¼ ç»ç¤¼ä»ª</span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen2012175_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen2012175-80">[79]</a></sup> and the birth of new forms of Confucian activity on the popular level, such as the Confucian communities (<i>shÃ¨qÅ« rÃºxuÃ©</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ç¤¾åºåå­¦</span></span>). Some scholars also consider the reconstruction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lineage_churches" title="Lineage churches">lineage churches</a> and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_temples" title="Ancestral temples">ancestral temples</a>, as well as cults and temples of natural and national gods within broader Chinese traditional religion, as part of the renewal of Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen2015a7_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen2015a7-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other forms of revival are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_salvationist_religion" title="Chinese salvationist religion">salvationist folk religious movements</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010203â214_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010203â214-83">[82]</a></sup> groups with a specifically Confucian focus, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_churches" title="Confucian churches">Confucian churches</a>, for example the <i>Yidan xuetang</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ä¸è½å­¦å </span></span>) of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010219_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010219-84">[83]</a></sup> the <i>Mengmutang</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å­æ¯å </span></span>) of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201529_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201529-85">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian_Shenism" title="Confucian Shenism">Confucian Shenism</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">åå®ç¥æ</span></span> <i>RÃºzÅng ShÃ©njiÃ o</i>) or the phoenix churches,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201534_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201534-86">[85]</a></sup> the Confucian Fellowship (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">åæéå</span></span> <i>RÃºjiÃ o DÃ otÃ¡n</i>) in northern Fujian which has spread rapidly over the years after its foundation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201534_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201534-86">[85]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestral_temples" title="Ancestral temples">ancestral temples</a> of the Kong kin (the lineage of the descendants of Confucius himself) operating as Confucian-teaching churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFanChen201529_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFanChen201529-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, the Hong Kong <a href="/wiki/Confucian_Academy" title="Confucian Academy">Confucian Academy</a>, one of the direct heirs of Kang Youwei's Confucian Church, has expanded its activities to the mainland, with the construction of statues of Confucius, Confucian hospitals, restoration of temples and other activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015148_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015148-87">[86]</a></sup> In 2009, Zhou Beichen founded another institution which inherits the idea of Kang Youwei's Confucian Church, the Holy Hall of Confucius (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å­å£å </span></span> <i>KÇngshÃ¨ngtÃ¡ng</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Shenzhen" title="Shenzhen">Shenzhen</a>, affiliated with the Federation of Confucian Culture of <a href="/wiki/Qufu" title="Qufu">Qufu</a> City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPayette2014_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPayette2014-88">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015152â156_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioudThoraval2015152â156-89">[88]</a></sup> It was the first of a nationwide movement of congregations and civil organisations that was unified in 2015 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kongshenghui" title="Kongshenghui">Church of Confucius</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å­å£ä¼</span></span> <i>KÇngshÃ¨nghuÃ¬</i>). The first spiritual leader of the church is the scholar <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing_(Confucian)" title="Jiang Qing (Confucian)">Jiang Qing</a>, the founder and manager of the Yangming Confucian Abode (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">é³æç²¾è</span></span> <i>YÃ¡ngmÃ­ng jÄ«ngshÄ</i>), a Confucian academy in <a href="/wiki/Guiyang" title="Guiyang">Guiyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>.
</p><p>Chinese folk religious temples and kinship ancestral shrines may, on peculiar occasions, choose Confucian liturgy (called <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å</span></span> <i>rÃº</i> or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ­£ç»</span></span> <i>zhÃ¨ngtÇng</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">orthopraxy</a>") led by Confucian ritual masters (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ç¤¼ç</span></span> <i>lÇshÄng</i>) to worship the gods, instead of Taoist or popular ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5-11">[11]</a></sup> "Confucian businessmen" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">ååäºº</span></span> <i>rÃºshÄngrÃ©n</i>, also "refined businessman") is a recently rediscovered concept defining people of the economic-entrepreneurial elite who recognise their social responsibility and therefore apply Confucian culture to their business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010204_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillioud2010204-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yushima_Seido_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Yushima_Seido_002.jpg/220px-Yushima_Seido_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Yushima_Seido_002.jpg/330px-Yushima_Seido_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Yushima_Seido_002.jpg/440px-Yushima_Seido_002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yushima_Seido_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yushima_Seid%C5%8D" title="Yushima SeidÅ">Yushima SeidÅ</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bunky%C5%8D" title="BunkyÅ">BunkyÅ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh" style="font-size: 115%;">å­æ°ï¼çºæ¿ä»¥å¾·ï¼è­¬å¦åè¾°ï¼å±å¶æèç¾æå±ä¹ã</span></span><br/>
The Master said, "He who exercises government by means of his virtue may be compared to the north polar star, which keeps its place and all the stars turn towards it."
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><i>Analects</i> 2.1 (Legge translation).</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>A key Confucian concept is that in order to govern others one must first govern oneself according to the universal order. When actual, the king's personal virtue (<i>de</i>) spreads beneficent influence throughout the kingdom. This idea is developed further in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Learning" title="Great Learning">Great Learning</a>, and is tightly linked with the <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> concept of <a href="/wiki/Wu_wei" title="Wu wei">wu wei</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans">æ ä¸º</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">ç¡çº</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">wÃº wÃ©i</span></i>): the less the king does, the more gets done. By being the "calm center" around which the kingdom turns, the king allows everything to function smoothly and avoids having to tamper with the individual parts of the whole.
</p><p>This idea may be traced back to the ancient <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanic</a> beliefs of the <a href="/wiki/King" title="King">king</a> being the <a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">axle between the sky, human beings, and the Earth</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperors_of_China" title="Emperors of China">emperors of China</a> were considered agents of Heaven, endowed with the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a>. They hold the power to define the hierarchy of divinities, by bestowing titles upon mountains, rivers and dead people, acknowledging them as powerful and therefore establishing their cults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146â147_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeuchtwang2016146â147-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucianism, despite supporting the importance of obeying national authority, places this obedience under absolute moral principles that curbed the willful exercise of power, rather than being unconditional. Submission to authority (<i>tsun wang</i>) was only taken within the context of the moral obligations that rulers had toward their subjects, in particular benevolence (<i>jen</i>). Confucianism â including the most pro-authoritarian scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Xun_Kuang" title="Xun Kuang">Xunzi</a> â has always recognised the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">Right of revolution</a> against tyranny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meritocracy">Meritocracy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh" style="font-size: 115%;">å­æ°ï¼ææç¡é¡ã</span></span><br/>
The Master said: "In teaching, there should be no distinction of classes."
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><i>Analects</i> 15.39 (Legge translation).</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Although Confucius claimed that he never invented anything but was only transmitting ancient knowledge (<i>Analects</i> 7.1), he did produce a number of new ideas. Many European and American admirers such as <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herrlee_G._Creel" title="Herrlee G. Creel">Herrlee G. Creel</a> point to the revolutionary idea of replacing nobility of blood with nobility of virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> <i>JÅ«nzÇ</i> (åå­, lit. "lord's son"), which originally signified the younger, non-inheriting, offspring of a noble, became, in Confucius's work, an epithet having much the same meaning and evolution as the English "gentleman."
</p><p>A virtuous commoner who cultivates his qualities may be a "gentleman", while a shameless son of the king is only a "petty person". That Confucius admitted students of different classes as disciples is a clear demonstration that he fought against the feudal structures that defined pre-imperial Chinese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another new idea, that of <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a>, led to the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">imperial examination</a> system in China. This system allowed anyone who passed an examination to become a government officer, a position which would bring wealth and honour to the whole family. The Chinese imperial examination system started in the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>. Over the following centuries the system grew until finally almost anyone who wished to become an official had to prove his worth by passing a set of written government examinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucian political meritocracy is not merely a historical phenomenon. The practice of meritocracy still exists across China and East Asia today, and a wide range of contemporary intellectuals â from <a href="/wiki/Daniel_A._Bell" title="Daniel A. Bell">Daniel Bell</a> to Tongdong Bai, Joseph Chan, and <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing_(Confucian)" title="Jiang Qing (Confucian)">Jiang Qing</a> â defend political meritocracy as a viable alternative to liberal democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>Just Hierarchy</i>, Daniel Bell and Wang Pei argue that hierarchies are inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> Faced with ever-increasing complexity at scale, modern societies must build hierarchies to coordinate collective action and tackle long-term problems such as climate change. In this context, people need not â and should not â want to flatten hierarchies as much as possible. They ought to ask what makes political hierarchies just and use these criteria to decide the institutions that deserve preservation, those that require reform, and those that need radical transformation. They call this approach "progressive conservatism", a term that reflects the ambiguous place of the Confucian tradition within the Left-Right dichotomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bell and Wang propose two justifications for political hierarchies that do <i>not</i> depend on a "one person, one vote" system. First is raw efficiency, which may require centralized rule in the hands of the competent few. Second, and most important, is serving the interests of the people (and the common good more broadly).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_A_2020_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A_2020-99">[98]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Against_Political_Equality" title="Against Political Equality">Against Political Equality</a></i>, Tongdong Bai complements this account by using a proto-Rawlsian "political difference principle". Just as Rawls claims that economic inequality is justified so long as it benefits those at the bottom of the socioeconomic ladder, so Bai argues that political inequality is justified so long as it benefits those materially worse off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bell, Wang, and Bai all criticize liberal democracy to argue that government <i>by</i> the people may not be government <i>for</i> the people in any meaningful sense of the term. They argue that voters tend to act in irrational, tribal, short-termist ways; they are vulnerable to populism and struggle to account for the interests of future generations. In other words, at a minimum, democracy needs Confucian meritocratic checks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>The China Model</i>, Bell argues that Confucian political meritocracy providesâand has providedâa blueprint for China's development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> For Bell, the ideal according to which China should reform itself (and has reformed itself) follows a simple structure: Aspiring rulers first pass hyper-selective examinations, then have to rule well at the local level to be promoted to positions as the provincial level, then have to excel at the provincial level to access positions at the national level, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Bell_2016_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Bell_2016-103">[102]</a></sup> This system aligns with what Harvard historian James Hankins calls "virtue politics", or the idea that institutions should be built to select the most competent and virtuous rulers â as opposed to institutions concerned first and foremost with limiting the power of rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>While contemporary defenders of Confucian political meritocracy all accept this broad frame, they disagree with each other on three main questions: institutional design, the means by which meritocrats are promoted, and the compatibility of Confucian political meritocracy with liberalism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutional_design">Institutional design</span></h3>
<p>Bell and Wang favour a system in which officials at the local level are democratically elected and higher-level officials are promoted by peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_A_2020_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A_2020-99">[98]</a></sup> As Bell puts it, he defends "democracy at the bottom, experimentation in the middle, and meritocracy at the top."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Bell_2016_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Bell_2016-103">[102]</a></sup> Bell and Wang argue that this combination conserves the main advantages of democracy â involving the people in public affairs at the local level, strengthening the legitimacy of the system, forcing some degree of direct accountability, etc. â while preserving the broader meritocratic character of the regime.
</p><p>Jiang Qing, by contrast, imagines a tricameral government with one chamber selected by the people (the House of the Commoners åº¶æ°é¢), one chamber composed of Confucian meritocrats selected via examination and gradual promotion (the House of Confucian Tradition éåé¢), and one body made up of descendants of Confucius himself (The House of National Essence å½ä½é¢).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Jiang's aim is to construct a legitimacy that will go beyond what he sees as the atomistic, individualist, and utilitarian ethos of modern democracies and ground authority in something sacred and traditional. While Jiang's model is closer to an ideal theory than Bell's proposals, it represents a more traditionalist alternative.
</p><p>Tongdong Bai presents an in-between solution by proposing a two-tiered bicameral system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> At the local level, as with Bell, Bai advocates Deweyan participatory democracy. At the national level, Bai proposes two chambers: one of meritocrats (selected by examination, by examination and promotion, from leaders in certain professional fields, etc.), and one of representatives elected by the people. While the lower house does not have any legislative power per se, it acts as a popular accountability mechanism by championing the people and putting pressure on the upper house. More generally, Bai argues that his model marries the best of meritocracy and democracy. Following Dewey's account of democracy as a way of life, he points to the participatory features of his local model: citizens still get to have a democratic lifestyle, participate in political affairs, and be educated as "democratic men". Similarly, the lower house allows citizens to be represented, have a voice in public affairs (albeit a weak one), and ensure accountability. Meanwhile, the meritocratic house preserves competence, statesmanship, and Confucian virtues.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion_system">Promotion system</span></h3>
<p>Defenders of Confucian political meritocracy all champion a system in which rulers are selected on the basis of intellect, social skills, and virtue. Bell proposes a model wherein aspiring meritocrats take hyper-selective exams and prove themselves at the local levels of government before reaching the higher levels of government, where they hold more centralized power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Bell_2016_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Bell_2016-103">[102]</a></sup> In his account, the exams select for intellect and other virtues â for instance, the ability to argue three different viewpoints on a contentious issue may indicate a certain degree of openness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> Tongdong Bai's approach incorporates different ways to select members of the meritocratic house, from exams to performance in various fields â business, science, administration, and so on. In every case, Confucian meritocrats draw on China's extensive history of meritocratic administration to outline the pros and cons of competing methods of selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>For those who, like Bell, defend a model in which performance at the local levels of government determines future promotion, an important question is how the system judges who "performs best". In other words, while examinations may ensure that early-career officials are competent and educated, how is it thereafter ensured that <i>only</i> those who rule well get promoted? The literature opposes those who prefer evaluation by peers to evaluation by superiors, with some thinkers including quasi-democratic selection mechanisms along the way. Bell and Wang favour a system in which officials at the local level are democratically elected and higher-level officials are promoted by peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Because they believe that promotion should depend upon peer evaluations only, Bell and Wang argue against transparency â i.e. the public should not know how officials are selected, since ordinary people are in no position to judge officials beyond the local level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> Others, like Jiang Qing, defend a model in which superiors decide who gets promoted; this method is in line with more traditionalist strands of Confucian political thought, which place a greater emphasis on strict hierarchies and epistemic paternalism â that is, the idea that older and more experienced people know more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Compatibility_with_liberalism_and_democracy.2C_and_critique_of_political_meritocracy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Compatibility_with_liberalism_and_democracy,_and_critique_of_political_meritocracy">Compatibility with liberalism and democracy, and critique of political meritocracy</span></h3>
<p>Another key question is whether Confucian political thought is compatible with liberalism. Tongdong Bai, for instance, argues that while Confucian political thought departs from the "one person, one vote" model, it can conserve many of the essential characteristics of liberalism, such as freedom of speech and individual rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> In fact, both Daniel Bell and Tongdong Bai hold that Confucian political meritocracy can tackle challenges that liberalism wants to tackle, but cannot by itself. At the cultural level, for instance, Confucianism, its institutions, and its rituals offer bulwarks against atomization and individualism. At the political level, the non-democratic side of political meritocracy is â for Bell and Bai â more efficient at addressing long-term questions such as climate change, in part because the meritocrats do not have to worry about the whims of public opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Joseph Chan defends the compatibility of Confucianism with both liberalism and democracy. In his book <i>Confucian Perfectionism</i>, he argues that Confucians can embrace both democracy and liberalism on instrumental grounds; that is, while liberal democracy may not be valuable for its own sake, its institutions remains valuable â particularly when combined with a broadly Confucian culture â to serve Confucian ends and inculcate Confucian virtues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other Confucians have criticized Confucian meritocrats like Bell for their rejection of democracy. For them, Confucianism does not have to be premised on the assumption that meritorious, virtuous political leadership is inherently incompatible with popular sovereignty, political equality and the right to political participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> These thinkers accuse the meritocrats of overestimating the flaws of democracy, mistaking temporary flaws for permanent and inherent features, and underestimating the challenges that the construction of a true political meritocracy poses in practice â including those faced by contemporary China and Singapore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> Franz Mang claims that, when decoupled from democracy, meritocracy tends to deteriorate into an oppressive regime under putatively "meritorious" but actually "authoritarian" rulers; Mang accuses Bell's China model of being self-defeating, as â Mang claims â the CCP's authoritarian modes of engagement with the dissenting voices illustrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Baogang He and Mark Warren add that "meritocracy" should be understood as a concept describing a regime's character rather than its type, which is determined by distribution of political power â on their view, democratic institutions can be built which are meritocratic insofar as they favour competence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roy Tseng, drawing on the New Confucians of the twentieth century, argues that Confucianism and liberal democracy can enter into a dialectical process, in which liberal rights and voting rights are rethought into resolutely modern, but nonetheless Confucian ways of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> This synthesis, blending Confucians rituals and institutions with a broader liberal democratic frame, is distinct from both Western-style liberalism â which, for Tseng, suffers from excessive individualism and a lack of moral vision â and from traditional Confucianism â which, for Tseng, has historically suffered from rigid hierarchies and sclerotic elites. Against defenders of political meritocracy, Tseng claims that the fusion of Confucian and democratic institutions can conserve the best of both worlds, producing a more communal democracy which draws on a rich ethical tradition, addresses abuses of power, and combines popular accountability with a clear attention to the cultivation of virtue in elites.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_17th-century_Europe">In 17th-century Europe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/220px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/330px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg/440px-LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LifeAndWorksOfConfucius1687.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Life and Works of Confucius</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Prospero_Intorcetta" title="Prospero Intorcetta">Prospero Intorcetta</a>, 1687</div></div></div>
<p>The works of Confucius were translated into European languages through the agency of <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit missionaries stationed in China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[note 2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> was among the very earliest to report on the thoughts of Confucius, and father <a href="/wiki/Prospero_Intorcetta" title="Prospero Intorcetta">Prospero Intorcetta</a> wrote about the life and works of Confucius in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> in 1687.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windows_into_China_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Windows_into_China-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Translations of Confucian texts influenced European thinkers of the period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mungello_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mungello-122">[120]</a></sup> particularly among the <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deists</a> and other philosophical groups of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> who were interested by the integration of the system of morality of Confucius into <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windows_into_China_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Windows_into_China-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Confucianism influenced the German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a>, who was attracted to the philosophy because of its perceived similarity to his own. It is postulated that certain elements of Leibniz's philosophy, such as "simple substance" and "preestablished harmony," were borrowed from his interactions with Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mungello_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mungello-122">[120]</a></sup> The French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> was also influenced by Confucius, seeing the concept of Confucian rationalism as an alternative to Christian dogma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epc_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-epc-124">[122]</a></sup> He praised Confucian ethics and politics, portraying the sociopolitical hierarchy of China as a model for Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epc_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-epc-124">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Confucius has no interest in falsehood; he did not pretend to be prophet; he claimed no inspiration; he taught no new religion; he used no delusions; flattered not the emperor under whom he lived...</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epc_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-epc-124">[122]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Islamic_thought">On Islamic thought</span></h3>
<p>From the late 17th century onwards a whole body of literature known as the <a href="/wiki/Han_Kitab" title="Han Kitab">Han Kitab</a> developed amongst the <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui Muslims</a> of China who infused <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> thought with Confucianism. Especially the works of <a href="/wiki/Liu_Zhi_(scholar)" title="Liu Zhi (scholar)">Liu Zhi</a> such as <i>TiÄnfÄng DiÇnlÇ</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©æ¹å¸ç¦®</span></span>) sought to harmonise Islam with not only Confucianism but also with <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and is considered to be one of the crowning achievements of the Chinese Islamic culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_modern_times">In modern times</span></h3>
<p>Important military and political figures in modern Chinese history continued to be influenced by Confucianism, like the Muslim warlord <a href="/wiki/Ma_Fuxiang" title="Ma Fuxiang">Ma Fuxiang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Life_Movement" title="New Life Movement">New Life Movement</a> in the early 20th century was also influenced by Confucianism.
</p><p>Referred to variously as the Confucian hypothesis and as a debated component of the more all-encompassing Asian Development Model, there exists among political scientists and economists a theory that Confucianism plays a large latent role in the ostensibly non-Confucian cultures of modern-day East Asia, in the form of the rigorous work ethic it endowed those cultures with. These scholars have held that, if not for Confucianism's influence on these cultures, many of the people of the East Asia region would not have been able to modernise and industrialise as quickly as <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and even <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> have done.
</p><p>For example, the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> on Vietnam was devastating, but over the last few decades Vietnam has been re-developing in a very fast pace. Most scholars attribute the origins of this idea to futurologist <a href="/wiki/Herman_Kahn" title="Herman Kahn">Herman Kahn</a>'s <i>World Economic Development: 1979 and Beyond</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other studies, for example Cristobal Kay's <i>Why East Asia Overtook Latin America: Agrarian Reform, Industrialization, and Development</i>, have attributed the Asian growth to other factors, for example the character of agrarian reforms, "state-craft" (<a href="/wiki/State_capacity" title="State capacity">state capacity</a>), and interaction between agriculture and industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Chinese_martial_arts">On Chinese martial arts</span></h3>
<p>After Confucianism had become the official 'state religion' in China, its influence penetrated all walks of life and all streams of thought in Chinese society for the generations to come. This did not exclude martial arts culture. Though in his own day, Confucius had rejected the practice of Martial Arts (with the exception of Archery), he did serve under rulers who used military power extensively to achieve their goals. In later centuries, Confucianism heavily influenced many educated martial artists of great influence, such as <a href="/wiki/Sun_Lutang" title="Sun Lutang">Sun Lutang</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> especially from the 19th century onwards, when bare-handed martial arts in China became more widespread and had begun to more readily absorb philosophical influences from Confucianism, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoism</a>. Some argue therefore that despite Confucius's disdain with martial culture, his teachings became of much relevance to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile hlist" style="width:19.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title" style="background:#ffe5b4;"><a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="padding-top:0.15em;padding-bottom:0.6em;;font-size:0.7em;line-height:0.7em;">

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:#ffe5b4;">Major publications</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content" style="padding-top:0.6em;padding-bottom:0.6em;"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Jeunesse" title="La Jeunesse">La Jeunesse</a></i></li><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Critical_Review" title="The Critical Review">The Critical Review</a></i></li><li><i><a href="/wiki/Diary_of_a_Madman_(Lu_Xun)" title="Diary of a Madman (Lu Xun)">A Madman's Diary</a></i></li><li><a href="/wiki/The_Tiger_(political_magazine)" title="The Tiger (political magazine)"><i>The Tiger</i></a></li></ul></div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="padding-top:0.15em;padding-bottom:0.6em;">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:#ffe5b4;">Notable people</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content" style="padding-top:0.6em;padding-bottom:0.6em;"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ba_Jin" title="Ba Jin">Ba Jin</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Chen_Duxiu" title="Chen Duxiu">Chen Duxiu</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Guo_Moruo" title="Guo Moruo">Guo Moruo</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">Hu Shih</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Lao_She" title="Lao She">Lao She</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Li_Dazhao" title="Li Dazhao">Li Dazhao</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Lu_Xun" title="Lu Xun">Lu Xun</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Mao_Dun" title="Mao Dun">Mao Dun</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Qian_Xuantong" title="Qian Xuantong">Qian Xuantong</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Shen_Congwen" title="Shen Congwen">Shen Congwen</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Yu_Dafu" title="Yu Dafu">Yu Dafu</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Zhu_Ziqing" title="Zhu Ziqing">Zhu Ziqing</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Qian_Xuantong" title="Qian Xuantong">Qian Xuantong</a></li></ul></div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:New_Culture_Movement" title="Template:New Culture Movement"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:New_Culture_Movement" title="Template talk:New Culture Movement"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:New_Culture_Movement&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Confucius and Confucianism were opposed or criticised from the start, including <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>'s philosophy and <a href="/wiki/Mozi" title="Mozi">Mozi</a>'s critique, and Legalists such as <a href="/wiki/Han_Fei" title="Han Fei">Han Fei</a> ridiculed the idea that virtue would lead people to be orderly. In modern times, waves of opposition and vilification showed that Confucianism, instead of taking credit for the glories of Chinese civilisation, now had to take blame for its failures. The <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> described Confucianism sages as well as gods in <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> as devils.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contradiction_with_modernist_values">Contradiction with modernist values</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">New Culture Movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lu_Xun" title="Lu Xun">Lu Xun</a> criticised Confucianism for shaping Chinese people into the condition they had reached by the late <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>: his criticisms are expressed metaphorically in the work: "<a href="/wiki/Diary_of_a_Madman_(Lu_Xun)" title="Diary of a Madman (Lu Xun)">Diary of a Madman</a>", in which traditional Chinese Confucian society is portrayed as feudalistic, hypocritical, socially cannibalistic, despotic, fostering a "slave mentality" favouring despotism, lack of critical thinking and blind obdedience and worship of authority, fuelling a form of "Confucian authoritarianism" which persists into the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> Leftists during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> described Confucius as the representative of the class of slave owners.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, there has long been criticism. Some South Koreans believe Confucianism has not contributed to the modernisation of South Korea. For example, South Korean writer Kim Kyong-il wrote an essay<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> entitled "Confucius Must Die For the Nation to Live" (<span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">ê³µìê° ì£½ì´ì¼ ëë¼ê° ì°ë¤</span></span>, <i>gongjaga jug-eoya naraga sanda</i>). Kim said that <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">filial piety</a> is one-sided and blind, and if it continues, social problems will continue as government keeps forcing Confucian filial obligations onto families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_Confucian_thought">Women in Confucian thought</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_and_imperial_China" title="Women in ancient and imperial China">Women in ancient and imperial China</a></div>
<p>Confucianism "largely defined the mainstream discourse on gender in China from the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> onward."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-134">[132]</a></sup> The gender roles prescribed in the <a href="/wiki/Three_Obediences_and_Four_Virtues" title="Three Obediences and Four Virtues">Three Obediences and Four Virtues</a> became a cornerstone of the family, and thus, societal stability. The Three Obediences and Four Virtues is one of the moral standards for feudal etiquette to bind women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Starting from the Han period, Confucians began to teach that a virtuous woman was supposed to follow the males in her family: the father before her marriage, the husband after she marries, and her sons in widowhood. In the later dynasties, more emphasis was placed on the virtue of chastity. The Song dynasty Confucian <a href="/wiki/Cheng_Yi_(philosopher)" title="Cheng Yi (philosopher)">Cheng Yi</a> stated that: "To starve to death is a small matter, but to lose one's chastity is a great matter."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey-136">[134]</a></sup> Chaste widows were revered and memorialised during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing</a> periods. This "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_of_widow_chastity" title="Cult of widow chastity">cult of chastity</a>" accordingly condemned many widows to poverty and loneliness by placing a social stigma on remarriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>For years, many modern scholars have regarded Confucianism as a sexist, patriarchal ideology that was historically damaging to Chinese women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> It has also been argued by some Chinese and Western writers that the rise of neo-Confucianism during the Song dynasty had led to a decline of status of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey-136">[134]</a></sup> Some critics have also accused the prominent Song neo-Confucian scholar <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Xi" title="Zhu Xi">Zhu Xi</a> for believing in the inferiority of women and that men and women need to be kept strictly separate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Sima_Guang" title="Sima Guang">Sima Guang</a> also believed that women should remain indoors and not deal with the matters of men in the outside world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Finally, scholars have discussed the attitudes toward women in Confucian texts such as <a href="/wiki/Analects" title="Analects">Analects</a>. In a much-discussed passage, women are grouped together with <i>xiaoren</i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å°äºº</span></span>, literally "small people", meaning people of low status or low moral) and described as being difficult to cultivate or deal with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> Many traditional commentators and modern scholars have debated over the precise meaning of the passage, and whether Confucius referred to all women or just certain groups of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further analysis suggests, however, that women's place in Confucian society may be more complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-134">[132]</a></sup> During the Han dynasty period, the influential Confucian text <i><a href="/wiki/Lessons_for_Women" title="Lessons for Women">Lessons for Women</a></i> (<i>NÃ¼jie</i>), was written by <a href="/wiki/Ban_Zhao" title="Ban Zhao">Ban Zhao</a> (45â114 CE) to instruct her daughters how to be proper Confucian wives and mothers, that is, to be silent, hard-working, and compliant. She stresses the complementarity and equal importance of the male and female roles according to yin-yang theory, but she clearly accepts the dominance of the male. However, she does present education and literary power as important for women. In later dynasties, a number of women took advantage of the Confucian acknowledgment of education to become independent in thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Joseph A. Adler points out that "Neo-Confucian writings do not necessarily reflect either the prevailing social practices or the scholars' own attitudes and practices in regard to actual women."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler_134-4"><a href="#cite_note-Adler-134">[132]</a></sup> Matthew Sommers has also indicated that the Qing dynasty government began to realise the utopian nature of enforcing the "cult of chastity" and began to allow practices such as widow remarrying to stand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> Moreover, some Confucian texts like the <i>Chunqiu Fanlu</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ¥ç§ç¹é²</span></span> have passages that suggest a more equal relationship between a husband and his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> More recently, some scholars have also begun to discuss the viability of constructing a "Confucian feminism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_controversy_over_Chinese_rites">Catholic controversy over Chinese rites</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Rites_controversy" title="Chinese Rites controversy">Chinese Rites controversy</a></div>
<p>Ever since Europeans first encountered Confucianism, the issue of how Confucianism should be classified has been subject to debate. In the 16th and the 17th centuries, the earliest European arrivals in China, the Christian <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>, considered Confucianism to be an ethical system, not a religion, and one that was compatible with Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elman_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elman-150">[148]</a></sup> The Jesuits, including <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a>, saw Chinese rituals as "civil rituals" that could co-exist alongside the spiritual rituals of Catholicism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elman_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elman-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the early 18th century, this initial portrayal was rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a>, creating a dispute among Catholics in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> that was known as the "Rites Controversy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunn_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gunn-151">[149]</a></sup> The Dominicans and Franciscans argued that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ancestral_worship" title="Chinese ancestral worship">Chinese ancestral worship</a> was a form of idolatry that was contradictory to the tenets of Christianity. This view was reinforced by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XIV" title="Pope Benedict XIV">Pope Benedict XIV</a>, who ordered a ban on Chinese rituals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunn_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gunn-151">[149]</a></sup> though this ban was re-assessed and repealed in 1939 by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a>, provided that such traditions harmonize with the true and authentic spirit of the liturgy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some critics view Confucianism as definitely <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">nontheistic</a>, in that it is not based on the belief in the supernatural or in a personal god existing separate from the temporal plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdler201412_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdler201412-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-153">[151]</a></sup> Confucius views about TiÄn å¤© and about the divine providence ruling the world, can be found above (in this page) and in Analects 6:26, 7:22, and 9:12, for example. On spirituality, Confucius said to Chi Lu, one of his students: "You are not yet able to serve men, how can you serve spirits?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinaiko_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinaiko-154">[152]</a></sup> Attributes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> were advocated by Confucius as necessary for social harmony; these attributes may be traced to the traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a>.
</p><p>Scholars recognise that classification ultimately depends on how one defines religion. Using stricter definitions of religion, Confucianism has been described as a moral science or philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> But using a broader definition, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_Streng" title="Frederick Streng">Frederick Streng</a>'s characterisation of religion as "a means of ultimate transformation,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> Confucianism could be described as a "sociopolitical doctrine having religious qualities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-153">[151]</a></sup> With the latter definition, Confucianism is religious, even if non-theistic, in the sense that it "performs some of the basic psycho-social functions of full-fledged religions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yang-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201034â36-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittlejohn201034â36_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLittlejohn2010">Littlejohn (2010)</a>, pp.Â 34â36.</span>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAdler2014">Adler (2014)</a>, p.Â 10: [...] Confucianism is basically <i>non-theistic</i>. While Heaven (<i>tiÄn</i>) has some characteristics that overlap the category of deity, it is primarily an <i>impersonal</i> absolute, like <i>dao</i> and <i>Brahman</i>. "Deity" (<i>theos</i>, <i>deus</i>), on the other hand connotes something <i>personal</i> (he or she, not it).<br/><a href="#CITEREFAdler2014">Adler (2014)</a>, p.Â 12: Confucianism deconstructs the sacred-profane dichotomy; it asserts that sacredness is to be found <i>in</i>, not behind or beyond, the ordinary activities of human lifeâand especially in human relationships. Human relationships are sacred in Confucianism because they are the expression of our moral nature (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">æ§</span></span> <i>xÃ¬ng</i>), which has a transcendent anchorage in Heaven (<i>tiÄn</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">å¤©</span></span>). Herbert Fingarette captured this essential feature of Confucianism in the title of his 1972 book, <i>Confucius: The Secular as Sacred</i>. To assume a dualistic relationship between sacred and profane and to use this as a criterion of religion is to beg the question of whether Confucianism can count as a religious tradition.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClart20033â5_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFClart2003">Clart (2003)</a>, pp.Â 3â5.</span>

<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFKaplan2015">Kaplan, Robert D. <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations_of_texts_attributed_to_Confucius">Translations of texts attributed to Confucius</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Analects_.28Lun_Yu.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Analects_(Lun_Yu)">Analects (<i>Lun Yu</i>)</span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://www.sacred-texts.com/cfu/conf1.htm" rel="nofollow">Confucian Analects</a> (1893) Translated by James Legge.</li>
<li><i>The Analects of Confucius</i> (1915; rpr. NY: Paragon, 1968). Translated by <a href="/wiki/William_Edward_Soothill" title="William Edward Soothill">William Edward Soothill</a>.</li>
<li><i>The Analects of Confucius: A Philosophical Translation</i> (New York: Ballantine, 1998). Translated by Roger T. Ames, Henry Rosemont.</li>
<li><i>Confucius: The Analects (Lun yÃ¼)</i> (London: Penguin, 1979; rpr. Hong Kong: Chinese University Press, 1992). Translated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.C._Lau" title="D.C. Lau">D.C. Lau</a>.</li>
<li><i>The Analects of Confucius (Lun Yu)</i> (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1997). Translated by Chichung Huang.</li>
<li><i>The Analects of Confucius</i> (New York: W.W. Norton, 1997). Translated by Simon Leys.</li>
<li><i>Analects: With Selections from Traditional Commentaries</i> (Indianapolis: Hackett Publishing, 2003). Translated by Edward Slingerland.</li></ul>
